Supplement to the Shen Bao Local Edition - Automotive Weekly

Amazing Achievements of Norton Motorcycles

— Fu

Norton motorcycles have always been known for their precision engineering and high speed, renowned worldwide. They have consistently participated in various competitions, invariably ranking among the top and winning championships. According to news received by the local distributor, Billiems Motor Co., from the factory, Norton has once again secured victories in the 1935 Junior and Senior Tourist Trophy races, as well as the Grand Prix races held in Europe. The excellence of their performance is truly astonishing, enviable, and worthy of constant praise.

It is noted that Norton motorcycles have now won the Junior and Senior Tourist Trophy championships for four consecutive years. Therefore, at the beginning of this Junior race, some people doubted whether Norton could maintain its champion status. However, the result was that Norton not only occupied the top three positions but even swept the top four in one stage of the race. As for Mr. Duncan's seventh-place finish, it was due to a crash.

At the end of the first lap, Mr. Guthrie led with an average speed of 78.99 miles per hour. The other three officially participating Norton motorcycles closely followed him. By the second lap, the top four positions were still occupied by these four Nortons, with Mr. Guthrie still leading the pack. At this time, his average speed had reached 79.05 miles per hour. Subsequently, in the third lap, the positions of the cars remained unchanged. However, the most noteworthy point was that Mr. Alerske completed this lap in 28 minutes and 19 seconds, achieving a speed of 80 miles per hour. It should be noted that no other junior class motorcycle has ever been able to reach this speed except for Norton. In the fourth lap, Mr. Duncan unfortunately fell behind due to a crash, as mentioned above. The remaining three Nortons continued to speed like lightning and safely reached the finish line. Their rankings are as follows:

First place: Mr. Guthrie, speed 79.14 miles per hour.

Second place: Mr. Alerske, speed 78.71 miles per hour.

Third place: Mr. Waytt, speed 78.19 miles per hour.

In addition, the team championship was also won by the Norton team. As for the Senior race, which was held for the first time this year, Norton's performance was even more astonishing, with an average speed of 84.65 miles per hour.

Campbell Returns to England in Triumph

— Yu

Achieving the desired 300 miles per hour

The origin of the famous "Blue Bird" car's name

Sir Malcolm Campbell, the world-renowned automobile racer, has recently achieved his ultimate goal of a speed record of 300 miles per hour and returned to England in triumph. Campbell's accurate speed record is 301.292 miles per hour on land. When the race had just finished, due to a timekeeper's error, it was thought that Campbell's speed had not yet reached 300 miles per hour, causing everyone to feel sorry for Campbell, as the failure was so close, but ultimately did not satisfy the baronet's desire. However, officials from the American Automobile Association notified Campbell late at night that there had been a timing error and that he had indeed reached 300 miles per hour. The crowd congratulated Campbell, and the baronet was also sincerely happy, saying that this kind of error was "funny." Campbell's car's speed averaged twelve seconds per mile. Initially, Campbell had planned to try again the day after the race to reach the 300-mile-per-hour target, but after the good news arrived, he immediately canceled the previous plan, packed his bags, and left the United States to return to England. The six-ton, 2,500-horsepower "Blue Bird" car also returned to its homeland.

The timekeeper initially calculated Sir Campbell's southbound speed as 295.566 miles per hour, averaging 299.875 miles per hour, which did not meet the target of 300 miles per hour. Later, after checking the electrical timing paper tape, the return mile was twelve seconds and eight tenths of a second, not twelve seconds and eighteen hundredths of a second. Thus, the initial record was officially announced as incorrect. Sir Campbell is tall and well-built, and his driving skill relies heavily on his physical strength, making him a true driving prodigy. Born in 1885, he initially worked for Lloyd's, in the insurance industry, in 1904, until the outbreak of the Great War, when he left to join the army as an aircraft ferry pilot. However, this was not his ambition. After being demobilized in 1919, he resigned, soon married, and the following year purchased a car to race at Brooklands.

Sir Campbell's wife is also very interested in car racing and can drive. In 1921, Lady Campbell thought that if a woman engaged in car racing, it would definitely attract interest. She told Campbell, "I will personally drive and race to see what kind of interest you find in car racing." A week later, Campbell returned and told his wife, "I have registered you for the car race at Brooklands next Saturday." Lady Campbell thought there was still plenty of time to practice, so she said to her husband, "Then you should lend me your car for practice." Before Saturday, Campbell went out with his wife. Lady Campbell drove two laps, with a speed of 80 miles per hour. Her husband told her that this result was not very good and that it would be very difficult to win the championship. She was very unhappy after this setback. On the day of the race, Lady Campbell, as a result of her efforts, reached a speed of 110 miles per hour and finished fourth. She did not find any fun in racing, so without waiting for her mother and aunt to stop her, she voluntarily decided not to participate in car races anymore. Now, when Sir Campbell drives, she always watches from the sidelines. Although she is not happy to do so, she often attends. Sir Campbell repeatedly tells her not to be afraid, and Lady Campbell also tries her best to do so.

The car that Sir Campbell drove to win the championship is called "Blue Bird." The origin of this name is quite amusing. One day, he bought a car and planned to go to Brooklands to race. That evening, he decided to go to the theater for entertainment. The play being performed was Maeterlinck's "The Blue Bird." Campbell admired this play immensely. The next evening, the night before the race, Campbell went to see it again. After the play ended and he returned, he painted his newly purchased car blue and named it "Blue Bird." On the day of the race, the paint had not yet dried and was dripping, but Campbell did not care and won the first prize. From then on, Campbell named each new car he purchased "Blue Bird" to mark his good luck. The new Blue Bird car used in this race was 28 feet long, and the shape of its tail was very strange. The gas used to start it up almost caused people to become mute, and those who smelled it suffocated, like the smell of trinitrotoluene when it explodes.

The tires used on the car were repeatedly tested beforehand to see if they could withstand the intense friction of 300 miles per hour. No less than forty tires were tested, with dimensions of 37 by 8, using ten to twelve layers of silk and canvas lining, with a thin rubber surface on the outside. All the equipment and methods used in the race were in accordance with the regulations of the American Automobile Association's racing group. The finish line had a red bullseye. When the "Blue Bird's" upward-pointing nose touched the pre-installed metal platform, it would be connected to the judges' platform for the official recording time. The tool for recording time was a current-powered paper tape machine.

After this final race, Sir Campbell will serve as the chief of Central Newsreel for the American Fox Film Corporation. Campbell is often seen in newsreels and should have considerable experience in this field. According to Campbell, the cost of this type of racing is astonishing. Because he inherited a large fortune when he was young, he was able to complete this work. He also said that when the "Blue Bird" is running, steering is the most difficult, often causing blisters on the palms of his hands. Campbell's life is dedicated to obtaining land speed records, and car racing is one of his private life's works. Otherwise, he would specialize in treasure hunting. He previously spent one or two years on Cocos Island, excavating gold and silver hidden by pirates in 1821, worth a total of $60,000,000, but was unsuccessful. When he retires to his hometown of Surrey, he will still use driving as a pastime, but his speed will be no more than 50 miles per hour.

A Car Trip to Northern Zhejiang (Part 1)

— Lingyun

Several Westerners in this city drove a Vauxhall sedan in late August for a week-long trip to northern Zhejiang. They recorded the itinerary and time in great detail. Here is a translation for reference.

On Friday, August 23rd, at 1:30 PM, we set off from St. George's Road in Jing'an Temple for a car trip on the Zhejiang Highway. The car we drove was a 1935 large six-cylinder Vauxhall, with 27 horsepower. The fuel tank was filled with 14 gallons of gasoline, and the odometer reading was 4740 miles. The first 133 miles of the journey, from Shanghai to West Lake in Hangzhou, are known to anyone in this city who has a Chinese car license, so I won't go into detail. However, I will still use a diary format to explain the important locations along the way in order. The readers will want to know the records of the time and itinerary.

We crossed the Xujiahui Bridge from Avenue Dubail in the French Concession. (The speedometer reading was 4742 miles.) At 2:10 PM, we arrived at the Minhang ferry. The road we passed was a cinder surface, rough and uneven. If we only consider this point, it can be said to be the worst road on the entire journey. At 3:00 PM, we arrived at the seawall, and the Zhoushan Islands in Hangzhou Bay were faintly visible. (4776 miles) A quarter of an hour later, the odometer reading was 4787 miles. The stone sign marking the border between Jiangsu and Zhejiang had been passed. From now on, the mileage markers along the road all indicate the distance from Hangzhou.

At 3:40 PM, we arrived at Zhapu. (4800 miles) This place is still a restricted area, and because the seawall is narrow, driving is not easy. There have been dangerous incidents involving Chinese people because they are not used to driving. The second checkpoint where car licenses must be inspected is Haiyan. (Arrived at 4:00 PM, 4811 miles) The inspection place is an ancient temple at a cape. A courteous official directs the operation. In this situation, it is best to present your passport and your business card. Of course, the car license and driver's license should also be presented.

Next is Ganpu, a small town. (It was a large world trade city before 877 AD. It was already famous in Marco Polo's time.) This town is located in a small mountain in the second district, which Shanghai residents pass through on their way to Hangzhou. After a journey of 133 miles, we arrived at West Lake in Hangzhou at 6:30 PM. (The odometer reading was 4873 miles.)

The next day, Saturday, we departed from Hangzhou. After filling up with eight gallons of gasoline, we headed to Huizhou via the Qiantang River Bridge at 6:45 AM. (4874 miles) From various maps, it seems that we should be able to see the Qiantang River along the way, but in reality, this is not the case. It is very different from the ideal. However, several places we passed through have excellent scenery. There are cliffs on both sides, and the road passes through the middle, which shows how difficult the construction was. After passing through scattered tea-growing areas, we arrived at Fuyang at about 7:45 AM. The odometer reading was 4899 miles. At 4904 miles, we saw some small mountains, which were very beautiful. After passing the Songxi River (4913 miles), we took a cliff road and advanced along the Qiantang River. At 9:30 AM, we arrived at Tonglu, with a mileage of 4931 miles. This place has small wooden ferries for crossing the Tongxi River, costing one dollar. However, one must be very careful when getting on and off the ferry because the angle at which the gangplank is placed is often very steep. At 10:00 AM, at a mileage of 4941 miles, we arrived at the branch road to Qililong. After another four miles of driving and one and a half miles of walking, we reached Yanziling Fishing Terrace. This is a famous scenic spot with a history of more than 2,000 years. At 11:30 AM, we returned to the main road with a mileage of 4949 miles. An hour later, we arrived at the Baisha fork in the road. (4749 miles) We proceeded on the road to the right. At 4989 miles, we passed the bus station. The toll for crossing the river thirteen miles ahead is collected here.

A History of the Diesel Engine (Part 2)

— Zhu Yisheng

If you need to inflate the rubber tires of your bicycle, you will feel the air pump casing getting hot after a few minutes of pumping. This is because the air inside is under high pressure. The diesel engine is based on this principle. When its piston moves upward in the cylinder, the air inside the cylinder is subjected to strong pressure, about 500 to 1200 pounds per square inch. This strongly compressed air also generates heat of 1000 degrees Fahrenheit. At this point, the oil injector pre-installed on the cylinder head injects a mist-like heavy oil. It immediately combusts due to the high-temperature air, explodes, and pushes the piston down, just like a gasoline engine using a spark plug to ignite gasoline.

Because the air pressure inside the cylinder is particularly high, the structure of the cylinder is much stronger than that of ordinary gasoline engines. This is the first difficulty, because the engine itself feels heavy. However, modern metallurgy, using specially made alloys and baking methods, has gradually solved this problem. For example, the Cummins engine only weighs 15.6 pounds per horsepower, which is not much different from the 14.9 pounds per horsepower of ordinary gasoline engines.

The second difficulty is that because the air pressure in the cylinder is too high, it is difficult to start. Ordinary gasoline engines can rotate automatically once they are driven by a motor. However, diesel engines do not need to use electricity at all, so there is no need to install a motor specifically for starting. If you use your hand to crank it, you will not be able to do it. Especially in this modern world, wouldn't it be ridiculous? However, in this era of scientific omnipotence, this problem can naturally be solved easily. For example, springs, cold air, or centrifugal flywheels can be used. Let us wait and see.

In addition, the difficulty lies in the fact that heavy oil is strong and not as supple as gasoline. Gasoline-powered cars are easier to operate comparatively. However, when the weather is cold, the diesel engine is not affected by freezing. This is its characteristic.

Behind today's large-scale manufacturing, there is a strange story. We may as well observe the evolution of the diesel engine step by step.

Sixty-five years ago, there was a blacksmith named Christian Jorgensen in Minneapolis, USA. He can be said to be the ancestor of the diesel engine. He installed two pieces of hardwood on the upper part of the engine cylinder and used a machine twist to make them rub against each other, generating heat and causing the compressed air to burn and explode. However, this engine was only experimental and not practical. A few years later, there was a mineral soil in West Virginia named Uhmlen who came up with the idea of building a coal powder engine. The only difference at that time was that heavy oil was not used. After several years of experimentation, it was quite successful. Unfortunately, the cylinder exploded later, and the inventor unfortunately died of serious injuries.

The diesel engine is named after its inventor, Dr. Rudolf Diesel. His parents were both German, but he was born in Paris, France. He studied science at specialized schools in England and Germany. After graduating, he once managed a refrigeration plant in Paris. In 1883, he used his ingenuity to create a special engine, putting the drawings and calculations on paper. Three years later, the ideal became a reality. However, his engine at that time also used coal powder like Uhmlen. In the end, it also exploded into pieces.

Fortunately, Dr. Diesel did not suffer any major danger. After coming out of the hospital, he planned to use oil instead of coal in his experiments. It was truly successful. At that time, an American businessman named Bushey bought his patent rights in the United States.

Although machine engineers everywhere held bright hopes and enthusiasm for his engine at that time, the cunning wealthy people always took a cold attitude and did not provide assistance. It was not until after his patent expired in 1912 that they showed interest in investing. Dr. Diesel felt the cruelty of the world towards him and threw himself into the English Channel in the year before the European War.
